In the long term, it is advisable to wait until the woman has had all the babies she wants in order to intervene especially for rectocele causing symptoms. Surgically, posterior colpoperineorrhaphy is usually curative. To</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 259 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>ensure permanent integrity of the rectocele repair, the patient should be advised to avoid straining, coughing and other strenuous activities and prevent constipation through a proper diet, plenty of fluid intake and the use of stool softening laxatives and lubricating suppositories.</p>
<p>Recurrence of the condition after adequate repair is uncommon if chronic constipation has also been corrected. Subsequent vaginal deliveries should be avoided, hence pointing to the need for elective Caesarean section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-of-any-complications-that-may-arise-from-rectocele-and-cystocele">Can you think of any complications that may arise from rectocele and cystocele?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-of-any-complications-that-may-arise-from-rectocele-and-cystocele" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some of the complications that may arise from rectocele and cystocele include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Leukorrhoea or increased vaginal discharge.</li>
<li>Abnormal uterine bleeding.</li>
<li>Abortion as a result of infection or disordered uterine or ovarian circulation in the prolapse.</li>
<li>Urinary tract infection is common with prolapse because of the cystocele and urethra obstruction with hydronephrosis.</li>
<li>Haemorrhoids due to straining with constipation, especially with rectocele.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uterine-prolapse">Uterine Prolapse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uterine-prolapse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A uterine prolapse is the abnormal protrusion of the uterus through the pelvic floor aperture or genital hiatus. Uterine prolapse can be classified into the following degrees.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260113-3c9587bc.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg">
First Degree Slight descent of the uterus. Cervix remains within the vagina.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260113-0aa51ff3.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg">
Second Degree Cervix projects beyond the vulva when the patient strains.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 260 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260113-410dd0e7.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Third degree (procidentia) The entire uterus has prolapsed outside the vulva</p>
<p>The most likely causes of uterine prolapse will be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Uterine prolapse occurs most commonly in multiparous women, like the cystocele and rectocele conditions with which it is usually associated.</li>
<li>In Caucasian women, it occurs as the result of gradually progressive injuries to the endopelvic fascia due to childbirth and lacerations of muscles, especially the levator muscles and those of the perineal body.</li>
<li>The condition may also be the result of pelvic tumour, sacral nerve disorders, especially injury to the S1-4, diabetic neuropathy, caudal anaesthesia accident and pre-sacral tumour.</li>
</ul>
<p>Additional factors promoting uterine prolapse include systemic conditions like obesity, asthma, chronic bronchitis and bronchiectasis. Also important are local conditions like ascites, large uterus and ovarian tumours. A congenital type is seen in newborn infants during vigorous crying or vomiting (this type is common among the Pokot in Kenya).</p>
<p>A uterus that is in retroverted position is especially subject to prolapse. Prolonged labour may play a causal role and the condition is said to be more common amongst those who lead a largely sedentary life, usually with individuals who are immobile and/or obese.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-findings-of-uterine-prolapse">Clinical Findings of Uterine Prolapse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-findings-of-uterine-prolapse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Symptoms of uterine prolapse include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pelvic pressure manifested by sensation of fullness in the vagina.</li>
<li>Low backache.</li>
<li>Uterus may protrude between the labia.</li>
<li>Cervix may become eroded and may bleed due to drying effect on mucous membrane, especially in the third degree.</li>
<li>Firm mass palpable in the lower vagina.</li>
<li>Patient complains of dyspareunia due to trauma on the cervix during coitus.</li>
<li>Leukorrhoea due to uterine engorgement.</li>
<li>Compression and distortion of the bladder by the displaced uterus and cervix may lead to accumulation of residual urine, which leads to urinary tract infection, urgency and dribbling of urine due to overflow incontinence.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-how-the-problem-of-uterine-prolapse-can-be-prevented">Can you think how the problem of uterine prolapse can be prevented?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-how-the-problem-of-uterine-prolapse-can-be-prevented" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Did you remember these points? You will need to do or advise mothers on the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>The importance of pre-natal and postnatal 'Kegel' exercises to strengthen levator muscles.</li>
<li>The need for early and adequate episiotomy during the second stage of delivery.</li>
<li>Traumatic deliveries should be avoided at all cost.</li>
<li>Prolonged oestrogen therapy for menopausal and postmenopausal women tends to maintain the tone and integrity of the endopelvic fascia and pelvic floor musculature, and can therefore, act as a preventive measure.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-uterine-prolapse">Management of Uterine Prolapse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-uterine-prolapse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This should include surgical measures as well as investigations on the haemoglobin level, blood group, urea and urinalysis to detect any other abnormalities and also as a preparation for surgical intervention. Give the patient antibiotics and dressings, especially for third degree cases, after which a vaginal hysterectomy may be performed. For first and second degree cases, and for women of a reproductive age, Manchester repair is carried out. This entails colporrhaphy and amputation of the cervix.</p>
<p>Medical measures will include:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 261 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Vaginal pessaries (inflatable doughnut) as a palliative measure if surgical treatment is contra-indicated or as a temporary measure to mild to moderate prolapse.</li>
<li>Oestrogen therapy (systemically or vaginally) administered to postmenopausal women to improve the tissue tone.</li>
<li>Dilatation and curettage may be necessary to investigate for malignancy if there is bleeding.</li>
<li>Vaginal creams and/or medicated tampons may be useful.</li>
<li>Urinary tract infection or cardiovascular complication should be treated appropriately.</li>
<li>Enema or laxatives should be prescribed to help with constipation.</li>
<li>Obese patients should be advised to try and lose weight.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prognosis-of-uterine-prolapse">Prognosis of Uterine Prolapse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis-of-uterine-prolapse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Vaginal hysterectomy with anteroposterior colporrhaphy provides excellent and permanent vaginal support and if good healing occurs, preservation of vaginal functions as well. Recurrence may result from unrepaired cystocele and rectocele or from occupational factors such as heavy lifting or straining.</p>
<p>You will now move on to the final part of this section and look at traumatic disorders of the genital organs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="traumatic-disorders-of-the-genital-organs">Traumatic Disorders of the Genital Organs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#traumatic-disorders-of-the-genital-organs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Injury to the genital organs may be as a result of gynaecological or obstetric surgery. Fistula may occur due to direct or indirect occlusion, as a result of angulation or obstruction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vulva">Vulva<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vulva" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Injuries may be in the form of bruises or lacerations. These will heal by themselves as long as hygienic measures are observed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="perineum">Perineum<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#perineum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The injuries here may include primary tearing involving vaginal mucosa, secondary tearing involving vaginal mucosa plus skin, or third degree tearing involving vagina mucosa, skin and muscles.</p>
<p>Tears are usually termed as 'fresh' within 24 hours of occurrence and can be repaired. After 48 hours they are deemed 'old' and they cannot be repaired until healed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vagina">Vagina<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vagina" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The injuries to this organ may be bruises or lacerations. If oozing is present, then a vaginal pack is required. Alternatively, the vagina may have to be repaired.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cervix">Cervix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cervix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Injuries here may be in the form of bruises, lacerations and/or tears. Bleeding which is oozing or active will need a vaginal pack. For tears with active bleeding, the area should be repaired under general anaesthesia because unrepaired tears will lead to cervical incompetence causing mid-trimester abortion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="uterus">Uterus<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uterus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The uterus can be perforated as a result of poor technique of IUCD insertion or dilatation and curettage. The rupture may be either partial or total. It can be repaired if perforated or partially ruptured but with total rupture, a sub-total abdominal hysterectomy is required.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fistula">Fistula<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fistula" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Fistula means 'a pipe'. It is defined as an abnormal, winding opening between two internal hollow organs.</p>
<p>The most common areas in the genital tract that may be connected abnormally are:</p>
<p>Vagina to bladder - Vesicovaginal Fistula (VVF)</p>
<p>Rectum to vagina - Rectovaginal Fistula (RVF)</p>
<p>Urethra to vagina - Urethrovaginal (UVF)</p>
<p>Urinary fistula however, can occur at many sites.</p>
<p>Study the illustration opposite.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260113-299c73bc.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Some common causes of fistula include:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 262 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Obstructed labour due to pressure by the presenting part, causing necrosis. This accounts for 85% of cases in developing countries.</li>
<li>Radiation therapy for gynaecological conditions, which accounts for 15% of cases (usually many years after treatment).</li>
<li>Disease processes, such as carcinoma in advanced stages of the neighbouring organs.</li>
<li>Chronic tuberculosis or syphilis.</li>
<li>Congenital fistula, that is, an accessory ectopic ureter, which may open into the vagina. This condition can be recognised in childhood.</li>
</ul>
<p>The main symptoms and diagnosis of VVF include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The patient keeps on complaining of constant dribbling of urine from the vagina and generally does not pass any urine by the normal route.</li>
<li>On inspection with Sim's speculum, the fistula is situated in the midline half way up the anterior vaginal wall. This usually occurs as a result of prolonged labour. Postoperative fistulas are generally higher up.</li>
<li>Small fistulae admit a probe with difficulty, but may be seen on cystoscopy. Large fistulae admit one or two fingers.</li>
<li>Coloured fluid (methylene blue) runs into the bladder through a catheter and immediately flows out into the vagina.</li>
</ul>
<p>This condition may also affect the individual patient in different ways. Some patients report that they do not experience any sexual enjoyment. In addition, there is the possibility of psychogenic amenorrhea and vulval excoriation with urine. The individual may feel like a social outcast.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rectovaginal-fistula-rvf">Rectovaginal Fistula (RVF)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rectovaginal-fistula-rvf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most of the rectovaginal fistula are as a result of unrepaired third degree lacerations of the perineum and posterior vaginal wall, or repairs that have broken down so that an opening is left from the rectum into the vagina. Advanced cancer of the rectum or vagina can also cause RVF but this is rare.</p>
<p>Common symptoms of RVF include:</p>
<ul>
<li>With small fistulae, only mucus from the rectum may leak into the vagina. If the fistula are larger, faeces and flatus escape into the vagina.</li>
<li>The patient will complain of feculent vaginal discharge.</li>
<li>An inspection of the posterior wall of the vagina and the use of a probe will demonstrate the smaller fistula.</li>
<li>Perineal excoriation due to leakage of urine and faecal matter.</li>
<li>Symptoms may also depend on the site of the fistulae. If on the lower half of the vagina there is incontinence, flatus, or fluid faeces while if on the upper half there is continuous passage of faeces per vaginum.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-vvf-and-rvf">Management of VVF and RVF<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-vvf-and-rvf" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>First and foremost, prevention is easier than cure. In most cases fistulae formation can be avoided by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensuring that labour does not go beyond 12 hours.</li>
<li>Frequent emptying of bladder or catheterisation during normal labour since a distended bladder is easily traumatised by the pressure of the presenting part, especially in cephalic presentation.</li>
<li>Control of infection and excoriation (use of infection prevention principles).</li>
<li>In the event of destructive delivery due to prolonged labour, catheters should be left in situation for 48 hours to seven days.</li>
<li>For VVF, recently formed fistulae will heal if the bladder is drained continuously for 21 to 28 days and for RVF, a low residue diet should be given for the same period.</li>
</ul>
<p>Where surgery is indicated, it is important that fresh VVF is repaired at once. However, if it is only noticed some days after the injury, then two to three months should be allowed to elapse before the repair. This allows local damage and infection to settle and urinary infection to be eradicated. Most VVF can be closed by an operation via the vaginal route.</p>
<p>In the management of a patient who is due for surgical correction the pre-operative care will include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Blood for haemoglobin level, urea, Intravenous Pyelography (IVP) for ureteric fistula.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Examination Under Anaesthesia (EUA) to detect the type of fistulae.</li>
<li>Dye test (methylene blue) into the bladder to detect site of fistulae.</li>
<li>Nursing care, including proper nutrition to ensure fitness for the operation.</li>
<li>The woman will need a lot of encouragement and support since it can be a very distressing time.</li>
</ul>
<p>After surgical correction, the following specific care should be given:</p>
<ul>
<li>Great care should be taken to secure constant drainage of the bladder to enable the repair to heal well. The catheter should remain in situ for 10 to 14 days.</li>
<li>Relieve discomfort, give analgesics.</li>
<li>Prevent infection by giving prescribed antibiotics.</li>
<li>Ensure proper nutrition with increased intake of vitamin C and proteins but low residue.</li>
<li>Ensure maintenance of local cleanliness through douching, enema and warm perineal irrigation.</li>
<li>RVF is repaired after a course of antibiotics to reduce bowel infection.</li>
<li>Also sterilise the gut with tabs cabbracol 500gm BD for five days.</li>
<li>Give enema on the morning of the operation.</li>
<li>After the operation, the patient should be placed on a liquid diet for two weeks.</li>
<li>Liquid paraffin 10ml tds for two weeks, followed by analgesics and broad spectrum antibiotics.</li>
</ul>
<p>Together with the above, ensure that you provide other general postoperative care, for example, regular observation and personal hygiene.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Once the fistula is repaired, the patient should always opt for elective caesarean section delivery.</p>
<hr>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="section-5-infertility-and-climacteric-crisis">SECTION 5: INFERTILITY AND CLIMACTERIC CRISIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-5-infertility-and-climacteric-crisis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This section covers both infertility and climacteric crisis and the menopause in women. The Kenyan government, in its policies and guidelines on reproductive health, includes infertility and climacteric problems as components of reproductive health in essential obstetric care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe infertility and climacteric crisis</li>
<li>Specify causative factors of infertility</li>
<li>Describe the management of infertile couples</li>
<li>Specify climacteric symptoms</li>
<li>Describe the management of a woman who presents with menopause</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="infertility">Infertility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#infertility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Most normal couples achieve a pregnancy within a few months of trying. However, failure to do so after one year may be arbitrarily defined as infertility, provided that normal intercourse is taking place not less than twice a week. But what is the difference between infertility and sterility?</p>
<ul>
<li>Infertility is 'the inability to achieve a pregnancy or to carry a pregnancy to term after one year of unprotected intercourse'.</li>
<li>Sterility is 'the inability to produce offspring, i.e. the inability to conceive (female sterility) or to induce conception (male sterility)'.</li>
</ul>
<p>There are two types of infertility; primary and secondary. In primary infertility, no conception has taken place at all. In secondary infertility, there may have been some conception even if it ended in a spontaneous abortion.</p>
<p>There are several causes of infertility, which will be covered here under three broad categories - general factors, female factors and male factors.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="general-factors-affecting-fertility">General Factors Affecting Fertility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-factors-affecting-fertility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="age">Age<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#age" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>In the female conception occurs at any time after menarche and before menopause. It is rare in the first few cycles and the last few cycles</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>before menopause because the cycles are usually anovular.</p>
<p>Fertility in women is at its height in the late teens and early twenties. It declines slowly after 30 years of age. In the male, spermatogenesis commences actively at puberty and continues throughout life but ageing reduces fertility to a variable extent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="health-and-nutrition">Health and Nutrition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#health-and-nutrition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Good health is associated with fertility but bad health is not an absolute barrier to conception, except when ovulation and spermatogenesis are directly affected.</p>
<p>Deliberate dieting (condition known as anorexia nervosa) will lead to loss of weight, which in turn will lead to amenorrhea, hence failure to ovulate. On the other hand, sometimes obesity and infertility seem to be connected in women, probably because obese women ovulate and menstruate less frequently.</p>
<p>Chronic alcoholism or drug addiction may also lead to infertility. For example, morphine tends to depress ovarian activity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="psychological-factors">Psychological Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychological-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Anxiety and tension seem to be responsible for infertility in some individuals. They manifest through changes in the neuroendocrine control of ovulation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="female-factors-affecting-fertility">Female Factors Affecting Fertility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#female-factors-affecting-fertility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some of the causes of infertility in females include:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Congenital, also known as Mullerian agenesis, where there is no uterus or ovaries. Another factor is vaginal atresia, which is the narrowing or stenosis of the vagina.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Infections leading to tubal blockage, for example, sexually transmitted infections (gonococcal, chlamydia), tuberculosis, salmonella, enterobias vermicularis and postabortal or puerperal sepsis.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Endocrine due to pituitary adenoma with increased prolactin leading to inappropriate galactorrhoea which is associated with amenorrhoea and anovulation. Hormonal imbalance with increased oestrogen will lead to endometrial hyperplasia hence irregular period (metropathia haemorrhagica) or prolonged periods and cycles. Hypothyroidism is generally associated with infertility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diabetes mellitus, if uncontrolled or in those with severe complications may impair fertility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Uterine fibromyoma, especially if it is large enough to distort the uterine cavity or block interstitial parts of the tube, may also cause recurrent abortion.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cervical hostility, whereby the cervical mucus is unreceptive to spermatozoa and either prevents their progressive advance or actually kills them. It may be due to infection or the presence of sperm antibodies.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cervical incompetence is almost always a cause of mid-trimester abortion and will lead to secondary infertility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Endometriosis because of the menorrhagia may lead to infertility.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="male-factors-affecting-fertility">Male Factors Affecting Fertility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#male-factors-affecting-fertility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following factors in the male may lead to infertility.</p>
<ul>
<li>Congenital, for example, hypospadias (ventral external urethra meatus) where the opening is congenitally malformed.</li>
<li>Undescended testes, where the testes either remain in the abdominal or inguinal canal or are retractile, that is, tend to go back in the abdomen. Take note that the temperature of scrotal contents is about 1° Centigrade below the normal body temperature. Spermatogenesis is impaired if the temperature of the testes is raised and this is often the case with undescended testes. The temperature can also be raised by frequent hot baths, the use of thick, tight underclothes as well as living at high altitudes for too long.</li>
<li>Infections of bacterial origin, for example, gonococcal chlamydia will block the vas deferens after healing with scarring. Viral infection, especially mumps, can cause orchitis, interfering with spermatogenesis. Therefore, a male child suffering from mumps should be observed keenly for this complication.</li>
<li>Auto-immune problems whereby the cervix can be hostile to the sperm of a man and produce antibodies.</li>
<li>Impotence where there is no erection for unknown reasons.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 265 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Ignorance of coitus and in some cases excessive coitus.</li>
<li>Less sperm count (oligospermia) or no sperms at all (azoospermia).</li>
<li>Endocrine disorders, for example, pituitary failure, adrenal hyperplasia or thyroid deficiency.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember:
Examine every newborn male at birth to detect undescended testes in order to prevent the problem of infertility later.</p>
<p>Management of Infertility</p>
<p>Infertility is a very stressful condition with a continuous cycle of hope and disappointment. Life centres around trying to conceive a baby and nothing else seems important. Partners blame each other and become frustrated and guilty. Patients should be managed by a gynaecologist, whose scope of intervention will depend on the services or technology available. The couple should be investigated based on their general reproductive history. Clinical examination of each partner is carried out separately and any worries should be elicited and confidential information obtained from each partner.</p>
<p>In the male partner the following should be done. Take a history of sexual function, erection and ejaculation. Any past history of orchitis or STDs should be noted. It is important to establish how often intercourse takes place, whether there is any experience of difficulty in either partners, discomfort or lack of satisfaction (the male orgasm which brings about ejaculation of the semen is essential for fertilisation).</p>
<p>A clinical examination should also be done and should include an assessment of the size and consistency of the testes and epididymis on each side. You should also assess for the presence or absence of varicocele or hernia and the size of the prostate. Plan for action after the initial history and examination will include semen analysis. This should normally be the first step in an investigation because if there are no sperms present in the semen, it is pointless to investigate the female.</p>
<p>Prior to semen analysis, in preparation to collect specimen, the man abstains from coitus for three to five days and a masturbation specimen is then collected. It should be received in the laboratory within half an hour. In semen analysis, examine colour (if sturdy, then it is infected), viscosity, bacteriology (if pus cells present), motility (progressive, sluggish or no movement), morphology (the ideal is 50%) and sperm count (40 to 370 million sperms per cc). Note that in Kenya 20 million is considered an acceptable sperm count.</p>
<p>If the serum contains sperm antibodies, the spermatozoa become immobilised when they come into contact with the cervical mucus. The test should be done three times. Once the seminal fluid analysis has been done and found to be normal, then the next step is to test for ovulation and tubal patency in the female.</p>
<p>In the female partner, attention should be paid to any endocrine abnormality. A routine pelvic examination should also be performed. During history-taking, the following should be ascertained:</p>
<ul>
<li>Any illness which might have caused peritonitis, for example, tuberculosis. This could mean the tubes were also affected.</li>
<li>Menstrual history, for instance, whether menstruation is scanty or irregular. Any abortion or sicknesses should be noted.</li>
<li>Outcomes of previous pregnancies should also be noted.</li>
<li>Marital history, including duration of marriage and how long she has been trying to become pregnant.</li>
</ul>
<p>The plan of action after taking down the patient's initial history and examination should include a Hysterosalpingography (HSG), which remains a useful investigation for tubal patency. A laparoscopy plus dye test does not only allow assessment of tubal patency but also gives proof that the tube is normal in appearance, the fimbriae are healthy and that the tubal movement is adequate.</p>
<p>Ovulation may be confirmed in several ways, including the temperature method, the examination of cervical mucous, progesterone level on blood sample taken one week before a period is expected (day 21 of 28 day cycle) and a level of more than 20 mmol/L, which confirms that ovulation has taken place. An ultrasound may detect follicular growth and ovulation. A histological examination of the endometrium should also be performed just before menstruation in order to assess the response to progesterone secretion by corpus luteum and to exclude tuberculosis.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 267 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment-of-infertility">Treatment of Infertility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-infertility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Not all couples that complain of infertility need to be investigated and treated. For short periods of infertility in a young couple, simple clinical assessment and reassurance that they appear normal is all that is required. Emphasise to the couple that if no abnormality is found, that pregnancy is always possible even after many years.</p>
<p>Treatment may take several forms. A detailed outline of ovulatory failure was covered earlier in section two. Tubal blockage can be treated by salpingolysis, which is the most successful tubal operation and consists of dividing peritubal adhesions around the ampullary ends of the tube.</p>
<p>Salpingotomy involves making an opening into the distal end of a hydrosalpinx and is usually better for fimbrial blockage. In tubal anastomosis, blockage, the isthmic portion is excised and the cut ends anastomosed.</p>
<p>During in vitro fertilisation, the oocytes from the mature ovarian follicle are retrieved, fertilised with the partner's sperm and the developing embryo is replaced into the woman's uterus.</p>
<p>In the male there are also various forms of fertility treatment, depending on the specific condition. Semen problems, such as azoospermia, secondary to lack of gonadotrophic stimulation, are curable by the administration of Human Menopausal Gonadotrophin (HMG). However, there is no treatment for azoospermia due to congenital anomalies or chromosomal abnormalities.</p>
<p>Synthetic androgens, for example, mesterolone (proviron), have a direct action on spermatogenesis. They should be administered at a dose of 50 mgs daily for three months.</p>
<p>Oligospermia will often respond to an improvement in the patient's general health and fitness, therefore, exercise and a good diet should be encouraged. The patient should also be advised to avoid excessive consumption of alcohol, tobacco and caffeine. Additionally, the excision of varicocele, if present, is commonly done for oligospermia.</p>
<p>Artificial Insemination (AIH) with the partner's semen is widely used for infertility due to impotence or anatomical abnormality in the male, especially in cases of hypospadias, which prevents normal ejaculation of sperm into the upper vagina.</p>
<p>You should also counsel the patient on general measures that may be helpful like refraining from excessive coitus, obtaining adequate sleep, weight-loss advice for obese patients and the need to avoid excessive and prolonged exposure of the scrotum to heat by avoiding hot bath, tight underwear or prolonged sitting in hot environments.</p>
<p>Some of the problems infertile couples may face will include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Poor social standing that is, not having respect from friends, relatives and the community as a whole.</li>
<li>The stress of infertility makes one be unproductive economically.</li>
<li>Sexual disinterest due to dyspareunia.</li>
</ul>
<p>The frantic effort of wanting children will complicate the infertility leading to domestic disharmony, separation and divorces and/or promiscuity which may increase the risk of STI/HIV.</p>
<p>As you come to the end of this sub-section, you will now be able to identify couples with infertility and assist appropriately. You have covered the predisposing factors in both sexes and looked at the investigations and appropriate management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="climacteric-crisis">Climacteric Crisis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#climacteric-crisis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In women climacteric crisis is the period of menopause (while in males it is known as andropause).</p>
<p>Menopause is a period in a woman's life when menstruation ceases naturally. There is progressive ovarian failure, which is preceded by complete absence of menstruation. Menopause is declared after one year of no menstruation. A considerable number of women will undergo physical or emotional upsets but the majority will not be significantly affected.</p>
<p>To some, menopause is a threatening period that declares the end of their femininity, while to others it is a time when many former diseases or ailments recur. A few will see this process as witchcraft or untreatable disease. You should note that sexual desire is present in most women for many years after menstruation ceases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="climacteric-symptoms">Climacteric Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#climacteric-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The climacteric symptoms occur as a syndrome. Menopausal syndrome refers to several symptoms related to hormonal imbalances in women. Menopause may occur without any symptoms other than the cessation of menstruation, but it is not infrequently associated with other symptoms. The most</p>
<p>common of these is the occurrence of hot flushes.</p>
<p>A hot flush is the flushing of the face and neck often with sweating. The flush may be only momentary or may last up to 15 minutes and recur many times a day. The flush occurs as a result of a rise in the peripheral blood flow (measured in the arm) and in the pulse rate, but no change in blood pressure. About 80 to 85% of women experience hot flushes. In Kenya these are often mistaken for malaria or typhoid.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong></p>
<p>Flushes are particularly severe and abrupt when there is a change in the hormone balance, for example, after bilateral oophorectomy and, in men, after orchidectomy.</p>
<p><strong>Climacteric Symptoms</strong></p>
<p>Other symptoms include weight gain in some women, which is generally a result of low-level activity. Exercise tends to promote hormonal balance. Osteoporosis can also result and has been attributed to oestrogen deficiency.</p>
<p>Atrophic changes in the vulva will cause discomfort and dyspareunia. A decrease in vaginal acidity may allow organisms to survive and lead to vaginitis or endometritis (this occurs in a small number of women). This may be confused for STIs.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong></p>
<p>Decrease in circulating oestrogen levels will lead to increased excretion of calcium in the urine. Calcium is essential in bone development.</p>
<p>Anxiety, irritability, insomnia and depression are often present in varying degrees and are seen mainly in women who have a history of psychological instability. Additionally, aches, pains, headaches, urinary urge and incontinence are often as a physical expression of anxiety and depression. Some women experience severe headaches for unexplained reasons.</p>
<p>Periods may cease abruptly or gradually diminish at the menopause. Although excessive and irregular blood loss is not uncommon at about the time of menopause, it must never be accepted as normal or 'just the change'.</p>
<p><strong>Management of Menopause</strong></p>
<p>The majority of women soon adjust to their new situation. This is especially true if they get support and understanding from their husband and family. For some, their problems may be</p>
<p>severe enough to warrant them seeking medical advice.</p>
<p>Oestrogen may be administered to suppress hot flushes and this may, in turn relieve sleeplessness and also help the depression. Some preparations that can be used include equine combined oestrogen (premarin) 0.62 mgs daily or oestrogen valerate (progynoval) 1 mg daily.</p>
<p>For atrophic changes in the genital tract, Dienestrol cream can be applied locally. Some doctors may give the treatment for several weeks to relieve symptoms, then gradually reduce the doses before discontinuing the treatment.</p>
<p>Long-term therapy (as long as ten years) may benefit the patient in preventing this condition, but on the other hand, may induce endometrial carcinoma or enhance the growth of pre-existing carcinoma of the breast. Therefore, it should be established as far as possible that no such tumour is present when this therapy is employed. Progesterone, if added, may prevent this.</p>
<p>Prophylaxis of osteoporosis is managed by increasing calcium intake. It is important for all women to know the importance of a balanced diet, especially plant foods, for example, fresh fruits and vegetables, which promote hormone production and especially soya beans, which have natural oestrogen.</p>
<p>The majority of middle aged or older women do not know what to expect at certain times in their later life, including the cessation of menstruation. Psychological counselling is often valuable and helps the patient understand the intricacies of the physiological changes that are taking place in her.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 268 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-6-neoplastic-disorders">SECTION 6: NEOPLASTIC DISORDERS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-6-neoplastic-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This section looks at neoplastic disorders, (more commonly known as tumours). You may find that here these two terms will be used interchangeably. You need to have an idea of the underlying pathology of these tumours so that you can identify them early and decide whether referral to hospital is urgently needed or not. You will also look at neoplasms of the genital tract and the breast (the breast is an accessory organ of the reproductive system).</p>
<p>This unit covers the types of neoplasms, aetiological factors, and prevention in general and then go on to a more detailed discussion of how each organ is affected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>List two types of tumours</li>
<li>State causative factors</li>
<li>Describe the investigations carried out to rule out tumours</li>
<li>Describe the management of various neoplasms of the reproductive organs</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumours">Tumours<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are two types of tumours:</p>
<ul>
<li>Benign, which are confined to a particular tissue. They include warts or multiple papillomata, fibroma and lipoma and endometriosis.</li>
<li>Malignant, which tend to spread to other organs near and far, for example, carcinomas that affect the epithelial cells and sarcoma affecting the connective tissue.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes-of-neoplasms">Causes of Neoplasms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes-of-neoplasms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The causes of neoplasms are not well known but they are associated with the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Drugs, for example, thalidomide, a tranquiliser once widely used, causes deformities in new born babies as well as tumours.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Radiation may lead to leukaemia (cancer of the blood).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Toxins, for example, aflatoxin found in cereals and groundnuts may lead to carcinoma of the liver.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Alcohol may lead to cirrhosis of the liver.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Smoking is associated with cancer of the lungs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>High parity women are reportedly prone to cancer of the cervix.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sexual activity, especially at an early age may also lead to cancer of the cervix.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The diagnosis is generally based on clinical suspicion, symptoms, signs and examination and a biopsy for histology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The management of neoplasms follows the same general measures that you have been looking at throughout this unit. Can you remember them?</p>
<p>The first step is to take a detailed history. Your investigations should include routine laboratory data like haemoglobin, urea, blood group, urinalysis and so on. You should also conduct an Examination Under Anesthesia (EUA) where a biopsy is taken to detect the type of cancer and staging.</p>
<p>Following this, treatment may take several forms. Surgery may be palliative in the late stages and in cases where the tumour mass is reduced. Radical surgery is performed in the early stages and involves the whole organ/system being removed. This procedure is sometimes curative.</p>
<p>Chemotherapy involves the administration of anti-cancer drugs. Radiotherapy is usually recommended in the advanced stage. More often, a combination of all the aforementioned treatments is used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Can this condition be prevented? There are measures that women can take which would help to curb tumours in their early stages and control them. These include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A pelvic examination once a year.</li>
<li>Breast examination (women can also be taught self-examination and are encouraged to perform this frequently).</li>
<li>Pap smear tests for all women of reproductive age once every year. For a detailed explanation of the findings of the Pap smear test, refer to unit four. Remember that Class IV refers to obvious malignancy.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 269 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Remember:
If Class III and IV are found in a woman of reproductive age, a cone biopsy is performed. If the woman is not of reproductive age then a sub-total abdominal hysterectomy is recommended.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Can this condition be prevented? There are measures that women can take which would help to curb tumours in their early stages and control them. These include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A pelvic examination once a year.</li>
<li>Breast examination (women can also be taught self-examination and are encouraged to perform this frequently).</li>
<li>Pap smear tests for all women of reproductive age once every year. For a detailed explanation of the findings of the Pap smear test, refer to unit four. Remember that Class IV refers to obvious malignancy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If Class III and IV are found in a woman of reproductive age, a cone biopsy is performed. If the woman is not of reproductive age then a sub-total abdominal hysterectomy is recommended.</p>
<p>Continuing below are more measures that women can take which would help to curb tumours in their early stages and control them.</p>
<ul>
<li>Sonography, whereby a scan of the pelvic area attempts to identify ovarian tumours and uterine fibroids.</li>
<li>Endometrial biopsy in post-menopausal bleeding after dilatation and curettage to detect cancer of endometrium.</li>
</ul>
<p>Now move on to look at specific organs and see the type of tumours that affect them and how to manage them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-tumour-and-their-management">Types of Tumour and their Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-tumour-and-their-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="tumours-of-the-vulva">Tumours of the Vulva<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours-of-the-vulva" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Genital cancers in women account for 5% of the reproductive organ cancers. The benign lesions (tumours) of the vulva include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Bartholin's cyst/abscess which is the most common.</li>
<li>Granuloma lesions which arise as a reaction to germs due to condylomata lata in third stage tissue reaction, tuberculosis, schistosomiasis and/or condylomata acuminata due to viral warts.</li>
<li>Very rarely carcinoma may arise in Bartholin's gland.</li>
</ul>
<p>Another rare tumour of the vulva is basal cell carcinoma (rodent ulcer), which grows slowly and does not metastasise although it infiltrates locally.</p>
<p>The symptoms and signs that are diagnostic in neoplastic conditions of the vulva are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ulcer or swelling, soreness or irritations are early signs.</li>
<li>Slight bleeding may occur.</li>
<li>Later, there is a purulent discharge, which becomes very offensive.</li>
<li>Enlarged inguinal glands may breakdown and ulcerate and occasionally severe haemorrhage may occur from erosion of femoral vessels.</li>
<li>Diagnosis is made by biopsy taken for histologic examination.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Early biopsy should be done in suspicious cases in women with leukoplakia and itching.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="prognosis">Prognosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The prognosis depends on the stage at which the condition is first diagnosed. It is usually worse when glands are involved. The size of the lesion affects the outcome. Lesions that are less than 2cm in diameter have twice as good a prognosis as larger tumours. If the tumour is first detected when it is small, with no palpable glands, then it can be completely removed with the glands and the patient has a 75% chance of surviving up to five years.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="treatment">Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>For cancer of the vulva, treatment will include examination under anaesthesia after which a biopsy is taken, and staging done. Surgery may involve the radical excision of the vulva and of the inguinal and femoral glands on both sides, especially if it is undertaken in the late stage.</p>
<p>Alternatively, an excision and biopsy may be done. Radiotherapy is performed on tumours at the fourchette because of involvement of the anal canal. This may be curative in the early stages.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="tumours-of-the-urethra">Tumours of the Urethra<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours-of-the-urethra" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A urethra carbuncle is a small, bright red swelling at the urethral orifice, usually occurring</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>in menopausal women. Some carbuncles cause no symptoms but most are very tender, and may cause dyspareunia or pain on micturition. They are not neoplastic but probably result from chronic infection of the paraurethral glands in the floor of the urethra.</p>
<p>Treatment is by excision or destruction by diathermy. Carcinoma of the urethra is a rare tumour but if it occurs, it is usually a squamous cell carcinoma arising near the meatus. It may sometimes be an adenocarcinoma arising in the paraurethral glands.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumours-of-the-vagina">Tumours of the Vagina<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours-of-the-vagina" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The benign tumours here include condylomata acuminata, which are also known as venereal genital warts. This is caused by the human papilloma virus and is transmitted through genital contact.</p>
<p>The originally small papillary growths tend to coalesce and form large cauliflower-like masses, which proliferate profusely during pregnancy. The growths may aggregate into a large mass that may even block the introitus during late delivery. Secondary infection is common. These venereal warts usually regress markedly during puerperium and may even disappear. The virus does not affect the foetus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The most effective drug is fluorouracil, recommended for use only during non-pregnant state because it is noxious to the foetus. The gravida with this condition should be treated by cryotherapy or excision of the lesions. Improved vaginal hygiene is also beneficial.</p>
<p>Fibromyoma is another benign tumour which may occasionally arise in the vaginal wall. It is hard and smooth and can easily be enucleated. Another possible tumour is endometriosis, a condition that has already been discussed in section two of this unit.</p>
<p>Carcinoma of the vagina is rare and, if it occurs, it is usually of the squamous type. Cancer of the cervix very commonly spreads to the vaginal vault. In the case of endometrial carcinoma, isolated vaginal metastases may occur.</p>
<p>Patients with cancer of the vagina complain of bleeding, especially after coitus and later, an offensive watery discharge. The growth may ulcerate forming a fistula into the rectum or vagina.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Treatment is often very unsatisfactory. Most cases are treated by local application of radium or caesium and external irradiation of the lymphatic glands of the pelvis. The only alternative treatment is extensive operation to remove the whole vagina, uterus and pelvic lymphatic glands. If the rectum is involved, it is also removed and colostomy established.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumours-of-the-cervix">Tumours of the Cervix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours-of-the-cervix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The benign tumours here are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Polyps</li>
<li>Fibroids</li>
<li>Nabothian follicles (which are often a result of infection)</li>
</ul>
<p>The carcinomas include adenocarcinoma, which is common, and squamous, which is less common.</p>
<p>Carcinoma of the cervix is a common genital malignancy. It begins in the cervix at the margin of the external os where it stays for sometime before it begins to penetrate the neighbouring tissues. It extends outwards towards the pelvic wall, downwards into the vagina, backwards to the rectum and forwards to the bladder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The affected patient is likely to be between 30 to 40 years of age and the signs and symptoms may present with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Post-coital bleeding because the penis rubs on the ulcerated cervix.</li>
<li>Post-menopausal bleeding. Any woman who resumes bleeding per vagina after menstruation had ceased should suspect the possibility of cancer of the cervix.</li>
<li>Offensive purulent discharge per vagina will indicate infection of the necrotic surfaces of the tumour.</li>
<li>Pain in the lower abdomen or back (this is a very late symptom).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="risk-factors-include">Risk factors include:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Early age (usually below 18 years) of first coitus.</li>
<li>Multiple sexual partners.</li>
<li>Viral infections like Herpes Simplex Type II.</li>
<li>High parity, that is, above five children.</li>
<li>Poor hygiene, whereby dirt collects in the vagina or in the foreskin of uncircumcised men.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As mentioned earlier, prevention includes educating women of a reproductive age to be aware of the signs and symptoms of the</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>condition, which will enable them to seek early treatment. Also women should be encouraged to take the Papanicolaou test every year.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Initial diagnosis should follow these steps:</p>
<ul>
<li>Take the patient's history</li>
<li>Physical examination</li>
<li>Perform a biopsy</li>
<li>In the early stage, a Pap smear test will help discover whether the condition is curable</li>
<li>Examination Under Anaesthesia (EUA) in order to conduct the biopsy and do staging</li>
</ul>
<p>After a diagnosis has been made, treatment should be given. Surgery involves radical hysterectomy in the early stages or may be necessary in combination with radiotherapy. Radiotherapy is either external (pelvis) or intracavity (caecium in the uterine cavity).</p>
<p>The prognosis is best in the early stages of the disease. Survival from the five year time frame and beyond is very poor. Complications of radiotherapy include relapses, ovarian failure, RVF and VVF.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumours-of-the-ovaries">Tumours of the Ovaries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumours-of-the-ovaries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
